    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d8d1ddac61a54972b90ae977ee55805d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '14338fe2-24fb-41de-b431-85756c5fabbf'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d113347950" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A fire-new “intrinsic distortion against Jahn-Teller distortion” mechanism in Na-ion layered Mn-rich oxides, that is, counteracting the Jahn-Teller distortion upon electrochemical cycling by introducing intrinsic structural distortion during the material synthesis due to their opposite deformation orientations, is unveiled to effectively stabilize the layered frameworks of the cathodes.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fc25a3d5-4b46-4712-bffc-c95f02fcbe2d/anie202423728-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fc25a3d5-4b46-4712-bffc-c95f02fcbe2d/anie202423728-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fafd44aa-e86c-4210-bc8b-4f710b16ef95/anie202423728-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fc25a3d5-4b46-4712-bffc-c95f02fcbe2d/anie202423728-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d113347960" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered manganese-rich oxides (LMROs) are widely recognized as the leading cathode candidates for grid-scale sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to their high specific capacities and cost benefits, but the notorious Jahn-Teller (J-T) distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> always induces severe structural degradation and consequent rapid cathode failure, impeding the practical implementation of such materials. Herein, we unveil the “intrinsic distortion against J-T distortion” mechanism to effectively stabilize the layered frameworks of LMRO cathodes. The intrinsic distortion simply constructed by introducing bulk oxygen vacancies is systematically confirmed by advanced synchrotron X-ray techniques, atomic-scale imaging characterizations, and theoretical computations, which can counteract the J-T distortion during cycling due to their opposite deformation orientations. This greatly decreases and uniformizes the lattice strain within the <i>ab</i> plane and along the <i>c</i> axis of the material, thereby alleviating the P2-P′2 phase transition as well as suppressing the edge dislocation and intragranular crack formation upon repeated cycles. As a result, the tailored P2-Na<sub>0.72</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode featuring intrinsic distortion delivers a considerably enhanced cycling durability (91.9 % capacity retention after 500 cycles) without sacrificing the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox capacity (186.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.3 C). This intrinsic distortion engineering paves a brand-new and prospective avenue toward achieving high-performance LMRO cathodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423728-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423728-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold great promise to satisfy the steeply increasing demands for grid-scale energy storage owing to the abundance and worldwide availability of Na resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0001_R_d113347937e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Layered manganese-rich oxides (LMROs) are persistently investigated as ideal cathode materials to promote the industrialization progress of SIBs by virtue of their high specific capacities, easy manufacture, and low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0006_R_d113347937e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, the Jahn-Teller (J-T) effect stemming from the high-spin Mn<sup>3+</sup> (t<sub>2g</sub>↑)<sup>3</sup>(e<sub>g</sub>↑)<sup>1</sup> would profoundly distort the local lattice structure and induce a large volumetric stress upon electrochemical cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0014_R_d113347937e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the collective and collinear distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>O<sub>6</sub> octahedra would result in the severe cooperative J-T distortion (CJTD), reducing the symmetry of the lattice structure and giving rise to the collapse of host frameworks and the crack formation during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0015_R_d113347937e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> This is widely believed as the major cause of LMRO cathode failure and urgently needs to be addressed to exert the intrinsic high energy/cost value of such materials.</p>
         
         <p>Aiming at circumventing this dilemma in LMROs, considerable attention has been currently focused on relieving or eliminating the J-T effect. For instance, low-valence cation (such as Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, etc.) doping in transition metal (TM) layers to reduce the Mn<sup>3+</sup> concentration could alleviate the degeneration of Mn 3<i>d</i> orbitals and thus mitigate the J-T distortion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0018_R_d113347937e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Besides, surface modification could restrain the internal lattice expansion/contraction upon cycling, favoring the suppression of J-T distortion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0026_R_d113347937e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Very recently, our group constructed the Mn domains with anisotropic MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra distortions in a Mn-based sodium layered oxide, effectively breaking the cooperative J-T effect and improving the cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0031_R_d113347937e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span> These approaches are certainly efficacious for strengthening the layered structures of the Mn-based cathodes, nevertheless, they either ineluctably compromise the redox-active role of Mn or strongly rely on the meticulous design of specific interface/crystal/local structural configurations, meanwhile, the sodium-storage performance of LMRO cathode materials has not been optimized to a practical use level. Theoretically, when the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox is participating in the charge compensation process, the J-T effect always exists and cannot be completely eliminated.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0014_R_d113347937e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a></sup></span> This fact highlights the necessity of exploring a counterintuitive but effective route to enhance the cycling durability of Mn-rich cathodes with general structures while allowing sufficient Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions and the presence of J-T distortions.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we first reveal the underlying “intrinsic distortion against J-T distortion” mechanism in LMROs, that is, counteracting the J-T distortion upon electrochemical cycling by introducing intrinsic structural distortion during the material synthesis due to their opposite deformation orientations, toward stabilizing the layered frameworks of the cathodes. Of note, this mechanism can function with no dependence on the elaborate design of special structures and without sacrificing the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox capacity. The intrinsic distortion is simply constructed by introducing bulk oxygen vacancies (Vo), which could neutralize the J-T effect within the <i>ab</i> plane and consequently uniformize the strain distribution along the <i>c</i> axis. As a result, the tailored P2-Na<sub>0.72</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode featuring intrinsic distortion (denoted as D-NMMO) delivers a high specific capacity of 186.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.3 C, along with an admirable cycling stability (91.9 % capacity retention after 500 cycles) over the wide voltage range of 1.5–4.0 V, significantly superior to those of the pristine sample without intrinsic distortion (designated as P-NMMO). Advanced in situ/ex situ synchrotron X-ray characterizations, atomic-level imaging analyses, and density functional theory (DFT) computations collaboratively demonstrate the suppressed P2-P′2 phase transition, minimized edge dislocations along the <i>c</i> axis, and delayed crack propagation in D-NMMO. The intrinsic distortion engineering opens up a brand-new and promising avenue to tackle the long-term J-T puzzles faced by Mn-rich cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423728-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423728-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The D-NMMO sample was prepared through an argon-annealing route (Materials Synthesis in Supporting Information), whereas the P-NMMO material was synthesized via annealing in the commonly used air atmosphere (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1a</a>). The stoichiometric proportions of both materials are confirmed by inductively coupled plasma (ICP) analyses (Table S1). The morphology and composition characterizations (Figure S1) attest the similar flake-like particles and homogeneous element distributions of both samples. The presence of bulk Vo in D-NMMO was systematically demonstrated by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR), energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) line scans, etching O 1s X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS), and electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS, Figures S2–S5).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0032_R_d113347937e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1b</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">c</a>) verify that both materials crystallize in the layered hexagonal P2-type structure with a space group of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>, and the detailed Rietveld refined results are provided in Tables S2, S3. The low-concentration Mg doping ensures the pure P2 phase for both cathodes (Figures S6, S7). Of note, significant difference in diffraction peak intensity can be observed between the two materials, which is clearly discernible by normalizing all XRD peaks with respect to the (002) peak (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1d</a>). The normalized (004) peaks, related to the <i>c</i>-axis only, show almost identical intensities for both samples, while the peaks entwined with the <i>ab</i> plane in D-NMMO exhibit apparently reduced intensities compared to those in P-NMMO, particularly evident for the (100) and (110) peaks that display a more than 50 % reduction in intensity. This suggests a weakened lattice long-range ordering within the TM layers in D-NMMO. Furthermore, the full width at half maximum (FWHM) of the (100) peak (~0.293) of D-NMMO is much larger than that (~0.172) of P-NMMO (insets of Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1b</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">c</a>), while the FWHM of the (002) peaks of both cathodes are relatively close (Figure S8), manifesting an enhanced <i>ab</i> plane micro-strain in D-NMMO according to the Williamson–Hall analysis (Note S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0036_R_d113347937e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> These results strongly signify a profound structural transformation occurring in the TM layer of D-NMMO. To elucidate the inherent structural changes, VESTA<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0039_R_d113347937e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">10</a></sup></span> was employed to simulate diffraction patterns based on Vo and TM layer distortion, given that Vo as a kind of physical defect can trigger the displacements of surrounding atoms and thus the lattice distortion. In the presence of Vo alone, all normalized diffraction peaks exhibit similar intensities to those of the perfect lattice (Figure S9). In sharp contrast, a merely 0.2 % distortion in the <i>ab</i> plane could substantially reduce the intensities of the correlated peaks (Figure S10), and the evolution tendency aligns well with the experimental observation for D-NMMO, hinting that the <i>ab</i> plane distortion is the root cause of the diffraction peak attenuation in D-NMMO.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bb225e1c-8abe-4108-9d70-384a1f5a841a/anie202423728-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bb225e1c-8abe-4108-9d70-384a1f5a841a/anie202423728-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/391624bc-557a-4c22-8b9c-d5961f147681/anie202423728-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bb225e1c-8abe-4108-9d70-384a1f5a841a/anie202423728-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Characterizations of the intrinsic distortion. a) Schematic diagram of the intrinsic distortion formation constructed by introducing bulk Vo via an argon-annealing route. Rietveld refined XRD patterns of b) P-NMMO and c) D-NMMO; insets show the magnified (100) peaks. d) Comparison of the normalized XRD peak intensities between P-NMMO and D-NMMO. HAADF-STEM images along the [001] projection and corresponding selected area electron diffraction patterns of e) P-NMMO and f) D-NMMO; the intensity profiles of line 1 and line 2, as well as the magnified local structure and corresponding intensity profile of line 3 are shown at the bottom of Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1f</a>, respectively. g) Simulated displacements of TM ions near Vo by DFT computations. h) Mn K-edge XAS spectra of both materials. Fitted FT-EXAFS spectra of i) P-NMMO and j) D-NMMO.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To directly visualize the distortion in the <i>ab</i> plane, high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) images along the [001] projection of P-NMMO and D-NMMO were recorded (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1e</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">f</a>). The standard nearest-neighboring TM-TM atomic distance should be equal to the lattice constant of <i>a</i>-parameter (~2.9 Å), as shown in the intensity profiles of the line scans in P-NMMO (line 1 and line 2). In contrast, the D-NMMO material features observable shortened (2.7–2.8 Å, blue lines) and elongated (3.0–3.1 Å, red lines) TM-TM distances, as highlighted in the magnified image and the corresponding line 3 intensity profile, thus leading to the diverse average TM-TM distances (Figure S11). The optimized structure model simulated by DFT computations (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1g</a>) reveals that the variation of the TM-TM distance in D-NMMO originates from the displacements of TM ions near Vo due to the lack of electrostatic attraction from oxygen at the Vo sites. The displacements are oriented along the directions away from Vo and the deformation distances are contingent on the coordinated TM ions (Figure S12). Consequently, the local structural distortions collaboratively lead to the global TM layer distortion (<i>b/a</i>=1.001), thereby weakening the long-range ordering within the <i>ab</i> plane and reducing the intensities of the associated diffraction peaks.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, we examined the electronic and local structures of both samples by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS). The average Mn valence in D-NMMO (+3.31) is lower than that in P-NMMO (+3.40) because of Vo, as demonstrated by the analyses of the Mn K-edge and L-edge XAS spectra, and the Mn 2p XPS results (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1h</a>, Figures S13–S15). Thereby, the compositions of D-NMMO and P-NMMO could be determined as Na<sub>0.72</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub> and Na<sub>0.72</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>1.99</sub>, respectively. The Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectra and the corresponding fitting results are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">1i</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0001">j</a> and Table S4. Compared with P-NMMO, D-NMMO presents a reduced average O coordination number of Mn (CN (Mn-O), 5.8 in D-NMMO vs. 6.0 in P-NMMO), further corroborating the presence of Vo. This is also supported by the reduced pre-edge peak intensity in the O K-edge XAS spectrum of D-NMMO (Figure S16).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0033_R_d113347937e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8a - Nat. Nanotechnol.">8a</a></sup></span> In addition, the Debye-Waller disorder factor of the second Mn-TM shell (σ<sup>2</sup><sub>Mn-TM</sub>), which is directly associated with the local structural disorder within the TM layer,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0019_R_d113347937e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Nat. Energy">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0040_R_d113347937e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> obviously increases in D-NMMO (6×10<sup>-3</sup> Å<sup>2</sup>) compared with that in P-NMMO (4×10<sup>-3</sup> Å<sup>2</sup>), further validating the existence of <i>ab</i> plane distortion in D-NMMO.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of P-NMMO and D-NMMO was evaluated in Na-ion half cells within the voltage window of 1.5–4.0 V. The charge cutoff voltage is set at 4.0 V to avoid complicating the Na-storage mechanism including the oxygen redox chemistry and the Mg interlayer migration (Figures S17, S18),<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0018_R_d113347937e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0043_R_d113347937e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> so that we could focus on the effects of Vo on Mn redox reaction. The discharge cutoff voltage is set at 1.5 V to take full advantage of the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox couple (Figure S19). Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2a</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">b</a> illustrates the evolutions of the charge-discharge profiles and corresponding differential capacity versus voltage (dQ/dV) curves during the first 50 cycles for both cathodes. The D-NMMO cathode affords the initial charge/discharge capacity of 92.7/186.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.3 C (1 C=180 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), considerably higher than that of P-NMMO (initial charge/discharge capacity of 66.5/163.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The superior Na ion (de)intercalation ability of D-NMMO is mainly rooted in the lower Mn valence in the pristine state, which allows more electrons transfer during the charge compensation process. In this scenario, D-NMMO exhibits a pair of conspicuous and highly reversible voltage plateaus at about 3.50/3.07 V, as verified in the dQ/dV and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure S20). This represents the P2-OP4 phase transition owing to more Na-ion deintercalation upon charge (as will be demonstrated later). Despite the presence of intergrowth structures in the high-voltage region, the D-NMMO cathode still ensures a lower capacity fading (0.48 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> per cycle) compared to P-NMMO (0.75 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> per cycle), which is an eye-catching and antiempirical finding. This is also supported by the more overlapped Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox peaks (~2.38/2.27 V) with smaller voltage polarization in the dQ/dV curves of D-NMMO. The cycling performance of P-NMMO and D-NMMO is depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2c</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">d</a> and Figures S21, S22. The D-NMMO cathode yields desirable capacity retentions of 91.9 % over 500 cycles at 5 C and 87.1 % after 50 cycles at 0.3 C, while the P-NMMO electrode only delivers 66.1 % and 77.3 % capacity retentions after 500 cycles at 5 C and 50 cycles at 0.3 C, respectively. Furthermore, D-NMMO exhibits a better rate capability of 80.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 32 C, in contrast to 57.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of P-NMMO (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2e</a>), which stems from the facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and accelerated electron transfer in D-NMMO (Figures S23, S24). As highlighted in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2f</a> and Table S5, the cyclic durability and rate performance of the present D-NMMO cathode compare favorably with those of the reported LMRO cathodes for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0021_R_d113347937e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Nat. Commun.">5c</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0046_R_d113347937e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> Aiming at further exploring the practical applicability of D-NMMO, a D-NMMO//hard carbon Na-ion pouch battery (5 cm×8 cm) was assembled (the hard carbon (HC) anode was presodiated before the full cell assembly), which displays a decent energy density of approximately 274 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total active mass of cathode and anode) and a respectable cycling stability of 91.3 % capacity retention after 200 cycles at 2 C (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2g</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">h</a>). The electrochemical performance outperforms that of the P-NMMO//hard carbon full battery (Figure S25). The Ragone plot in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2i</a> based on the rate capability (Figure S26) underscores the great superiority of the D-NMMO//hard carbon full battery in terms of energy and power densities over other recently reported sodium-ion full cells.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0031_R_d113347937e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0052_R_d113347937e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> In addition, the D-NMMO material exhibits an excellent air stability (Figure S27).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c591a7f3-9167-4793-a298-3f25b118eb72/anie202423728-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c591a7f3-9167-4793-a298-3f25b118eb72/anie202423728-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bcf930b2-74c9-49c5-8c4f-871ae5401608/anie202423728-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c591a7f3-9167-4793-a298-3f25b118eb72/anie202423728-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance. Galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles at 0.3 C within 1.5–4.0 V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and corresponding dQ/dV curves of a) P-NMMO and b) D-NMMO. c,d) Cycling performance and e) rate capability of P-NMMO and D-NMMO. f) Comparison of cycling stability of this work with previously reported LMRO cathodes for SIBs. g) Charge–discharge curves at 0.3 C within 1.4–3.9 V and h) cycling performance at 2 C after the preactivation at 0.3 C for 5 cycles of the D-NMMO//hard carbon full battery; insets present some working bulbs powered by the pouch cell and mass production of the D-NMMO material. i) Ragone plots of the present and recently reported Na-ion full cell systems.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The charge compensation mechanisms and local structural evolutions of both cathodes are revealed by ex situ Mn K-edge XAS spectra. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">3a</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">b</a>, the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox is responsible for the charge compensation during the Na-ion (de)intercalation process, and the larger half-height energy shifts of D-NMMO (0.98/1.47 eV for the charge/discharge process) than those of P-DMMO (0.76/1.33 eV for the charge/discharge process) account for the higher charge/discharge capacities during cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0020_R_d113347937e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5b - Energy Storage Mater.">5b</a></sup></span> The FT-EXAFS results are extracted to provide the detailed coordination information of the Mn-O and Mn-TM bonds in different voltage states, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">3c</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">d</a>. When charged to 4.0 V, the Mn-O and Mn-TM shells of both cathodes shrink, accompanied by the increase in peak intensity due to the oxidation of Mn<sup>3+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0021_R_d113347937e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Nat. Commun.">5c</a></sup></span> During the sodiation process, however, the Mn-O and Mn-TM peaks of P-NMMO display more significant attenuation and the peak intensities in the fully discharged state are even lower than those of D-NMMO (Figure S28a), indicating a more pronounced distortion occurring in the Mn coordination environment of P-NMMO (considering the higher Mn valence in P-NMMO than that in D-NMMO at 1.5 V, Figure S28b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0019_R_d113347937e1039" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Nat. Energy">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0060_R_d113347937e1039" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span> This is rooted in the more violent J-T distortion in P-NMMO compared to D-NMMO. The Mn-O and Mn-TM peak intensities are also visualized in the wavelet transform (WT) spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">3e</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0003">f</a>, the red and green colors represent the high and low intensities, respectively),<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0019_R_d113347937e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Nat. Energy">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202423728-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0061_R_d113347937e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Nano">16</a></sup></span> demonstrating the suppressed local distortion in D-NMMO upon discharging to 1.5 V. The above observations hint the critical role of the intrinsic distortion in neutralizing the J-T distortion.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/165f4a2e-02ee-43d7-8389-f65ba60e3b41/anie202423728-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/165f4a2e-02ee-43d7-8389-f65ba60e3b41/anie202423728-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c77a3825-ee82-452b-a104-7393e3f27037/anie202423728-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/165f4a2e-02ee-43d7-8389-f65ba60e3b41/anie202423728-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Charge compensation mechanisms and local structural evolutions. Mn K-edge XAS spectra and corresponding FT-EXAFS and WT-EXAFS results of a,c,e) P-NMMO and b,d,f) D-NMMO in different voltage states.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In situ XRD was performed to monitor the crystal structural evolutions of the P-NMMO and D-NMMO cathodes upon sodiation/desodiation (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4a</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">b</a>), unraveling significantly different Na-storage behaviors. Upon charging, D-NMMO undergoes a typical P2-OP4 phase transition in the deep state of charge (SOC) due to sufficient Na-ion deintercalation, while P-NMMO maintains the P2 phase throughout the whole SOC. The moderate rightward shift of the (002) peak of P-NMMO in the deep SOC is attributed to the slight gliding of TM layers,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0052_R_d113347937e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> accounting for the faint and reversible redox peaks occurring at around 3.50/3.07 V in the dQ/dV curves (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0002">2a</a>). It is important to note that the OP4 intergrowth structure can still ensure the stability of the layered framework, while the formation of the OP4-O2 intergrowth phase would lead to irreversible and severe structural degradation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0019_R_d113347937e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Nat. Energy">5a</a></sup></span> Therefore, highly reversible OP4-P2 phase transition during the preliminary discharge stage of 4.0–3.0 V is demonstrated for the D-NMMO electrode. With the progressive intercalation of Na ions from 3.0 to 1.9 V, the (00<i>l</i>) peaks of D-NMMO routinely shift to higher-angle regions accompanied by the gradual decrease in peak intensity due to the weakened O−O interlayer electrostatic repulsion and the formation of stacking faults.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0062_R_d113347937e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Unexpectedly, the intensity of the (004) peak of P-NMMO first increases and then decreases as the peak continuously shifts rightward (yellow rectangles in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4a</a>), whereas the (002) peak intensity shows the similar decrease trend to that of D-NMMO (Figure S29). Such an unconventional evolution of the (004) peak reappears during the second charge process, which is strongly associated with the generation of edge dislocations along the <i>c</i> axis (as will be demonstrated later). When further discharged to 1.5 V, a P2-P′2 phase transition occurs in both cathodes given the visible splitting of the (00<i>l</i>) peaks, attributable to the CJTD induced by the high Mn<sup>3+</sup> concentration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0063_R_d113347937e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span> The weaker P′2 phase signals of D-NMMO compared to those of P-NMMO suggest the much milder phase transition, confirming the suppression of CJTD. For the <i>a</i>-parameter, we chose the (012) peak as a better indicator to probe its variation, considering the featureless (100) peak in the deep sodiation state and the already known <i>c</i>-parameter change. A zigzag shift of the (012) peak can be detected during the discharge and following charge processes of D-NMMO (highlighted by the red rectangle in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4b</a>), reflecting the abnormal contraction (expansion) of the <i>a</i>-parameter with the reduction (oxidation) of Mn ions. By contrast, the <i>a</i>-parameter of P-NMMO varies monotonically upon charge and discharge. The evolution of the lattice parameters is extracted by Rietveld refinements, as presented in the right panels of Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4a</a>,<a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">b</a>. Despite the much higher charge/discharge capacity of D-NMMO than that of P-NMMO, the variations of the <i>a</i>-parameter for both cathodes are comparable. More impressively, D-NMMO possesses a smaller <i>a</i>-parameter (~2.927 Å) than that (~2.934 Å) of P-NMMO at 1.5 V despite the lower Mn valence of D-NMMO in the fully discharged state, further verifying the reduced <i>ab</i> plane distortion in D-NMMO. In addition, the more reversible lattice parameter evolutions demonstrate the higher structural stability of D-NMMO.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1d6bd8a7-44b6-4347-96a2-848960590934/anie202423728-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1d6bd8a7-44b6-4347-96a2-848960590934/anie202423728-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/912c93b7-d12b-456e-ba83-84857222e4c4/anie202423728-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1d6bd8a7-44b6-4347-96a2-848960590934/anie202423728-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Crystal structural evolutions and micro-strain analyses. In situ XRD patterns and corresponding lattice parameter variations of a) P-NMMO and b) D-NMMO. HAADF-STEM images along the [001] projection of c) P-NMMO and d) D-NMMO, the projection is determined by the inset fast Fourier transform (FFT) patterns, with scale bars of 2 nm; HAADF-STEM images along the [100] projection and corresponding GPA patterns of e) P-NMMO and f) D-NMMO. Ex situ XRD patterns of g) P-NMMO and h) D-NMMO when discharged to 3.0, 2.1, and 1.5 V during the initial discharge process, insets show the magnified changes of the (100) peak for both cathodes; i) FWHM changes of the (002) and (100) peaks of P-NMMO and D-NMMO.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further investigate the abnormal <i>a</i>-parameter change of D-NMMO and the uncommon (004) peak intensity evolution of P-NMMO during the sodiation process, we collected the HAADF-STEM images along the [001] and [100] projections in the states of discharging to 3.0, 2.1, and 1.5 V. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4c</a> and Figure S30, the regular <i>ab</i> plane can be probed in P-NMMO at 3.0 V, while D-NMMO shows a global TM layer deformation due to the intrinsic distortion (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure S31). Upon discharging to 2.1 V, the TM-TM distance in P-NMMO displays anisotropic elongation because of the potential CJTD,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0064_R_d113347937e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span> and this distortion is further aggravated at 1.5 V. In sharp contrast, the intrinsic <i>ab</i> plane deformation of D-NMMO almost diminishes when discharged to 2.1 V, manifesting that the CJTD is counteracted by the intrinsic distortion. It is anticipated that a possible reduction of <i>a</i>-parameter could occur under the neutralizing effect between these two distortions, accounting for the abnormal <i>a</i>-parameter decrease of D-NMMO upon discharge. As a result, D-NMMO features a weaker <i>ab</i> plane distortion in the Na-rich state compared with P-NMMO, coinciding well with the FT/WT-EXAFS results. In regard to the TM interlayer, it is found that apparent edge dislocations form along the <i>c</i> axis in P-NMMO during the sodium insertion process (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4e</a>), and the lattice strain (estimated by geometric phase analysis (GPA)) around these dislocations is especially intense.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0022_R_d113347937e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5d - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5d</a></sup></span> The dislocations are probably induced by the severe CJTD and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (as will be discussed later),<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0037_R_d113347937e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9a - Nat. Energy">9a</a></sup></span> thus triggering the unusual intensity change of the (004) peak of P-NMMO. Consequently, in the fully sodiated state, the TM layers of P-NMMO are severely distorted and misaligned driven by the strain inhomogeneity, resulting in the <i>ab</i> plane twisting and intragranular crack formation after repeated cycles (Figure S32). By contrast, the D-NMMO cathode maintains a relatively intact layered framework with small and uniform lattice strain during discharge (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4f</a>), thereby delaying crack propagation and ensuring structural robustness upon cycling (Figure S33).</p>
         
         <p>The micro-strain in both cathodes upon sodiation is also examined by ex situ XRD based on the Williamson-Hall method (Note S1).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0065_R_d113347937e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span> The broadening of diffraction peaks is ascribed to decreased particle size or increased micro-strain.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0038_R_d113347937e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - Acta Metall.">9b</a></sup></span> Given the similar particle sizes of both materials (Figure S1), the micro-strain is approximately proportional to the FWHM of diffraction peaks. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4g</a>-i, the (00<i>l</i>) peaks of the P2 phase continuously broaden for both cathodes during discharge, and the more rapid broadening for P-NMMO compared with D-NMMO indicates a more intense micro-strain accumulation along the <i>c</i> axis, consistent with the GPA analyses. This is attributed to the formation of edge dislocations. As for the <i>ab</i> plane, the FWHM of the (100) peak of D-NMMO slightly decreases when discharged from 3.0 to 2.1 V, and then increases after further discharged to 1.5 V. This verifies that the intrinsic distortion would neutralize the J-T distortion and thus moderate the strain in D-NMMO. In comparison, the <i>ab</i> plane micro-strain (or FWHM of the (100) peak) of P-NMMO increases monotonically and more dramatically due to the constantly aggravated CJTD. Post-mortem scanning electron microscopy images (Figure S34) confirm the presence of numerous particle cracks in P-NMMO after cycling, while the particles of D-NMMO are still relatively intact. This is responsible for the better cycling stability of the D-NMMO cathode. In addition, the Mn dissolution from D-NMMO is negligible upon repetitive sodiation/desodiation processes (Figure S35 and Table S6).</p>
         
         <p>The underlying correlation between intrinsic distortion and J-T distortion is further revealed by DFT computations employing the 3×3×1 supercells of Na<sub>18</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>36</sub> (without Vo) and Na<sub>18</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>35</sub> (with Vo). To visually reflect the degree of J-T effect, the distortion index DI(Mn−O) related to the dispersion degree of Mn−O bond lengths in Mn−O polyhedra (definition is given in Computations Details) is adopted.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0066_R_d113347937e1243" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Matter">21</a></sup></span> Compared to the fully sodiated lattice without Vo that shows an average DI(Mn−O) value of 0.070 (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0005">5a</a>, Figure S36 and Table S7), the fully sodiated latticefeaturing Vo exhibits a weaker J-T distortion confirmed by the lower average DI(Mn−O) value of 0.049 (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0005">5b</a> and Table S8), thus leading to a less pronounced <i>ab</i> plane distortion (Table S9), in good coincidence with the in situ XRD and ex situ HADDF-STEM results. During the simulated sodiation process from the Na<sub>0</sub> to Na<sub>18</sub> states, the average Mn-Mn distance near Vo obviously decreases while the average Mn-Mn distance far from Vo significantly increases resulting from the reduction of Mn ions (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0005">5c</a>, Figures S12 and S37), unambiguously indicating that the J-T distortions thermodynamically occur along the direction toward the Vo site owing to the low steric hindrance (right panel in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0005">5c</a>), namely, the intrinsic distortion and J-T distortion show the nearly opposite deformation orientations. This favors the neutralization of the J-T distortions during the Na<sup>+</sup> uptake process, reasonably accounting for the smaller <i>ab</i> plane deformation in D-NMMO (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4d</a>). The effect of CJTD on Na<sup>+</sup> mobility in the Na-rich state was also evaluated by calculating the mean square displacements (MSD) of Na ions (Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure S38). In the structure with the presence of CJTD, the Na-ion transport behavior is obviously diminished, manifesting that the distortion of TM layers will impede the Na<sup>+</sup> migration in the neighboring Na layers. This would result in a rapid lattice strain accumulation,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0037_R_d113347937e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9a - Nat. Energy">9a</a></sup></span> which is responsible for the edge dislocation formation in P-NMMO during the deep sodiation process.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8ccaf087-1e1b-43f0-9da5-a50e6a539db2/anie202423728-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8ccaf087-1e1b-43f0-9da5-a50e6a539db2/anie202423728-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0a325777-2ce7-47db-9eaa-4c8729834fcd/anie202423728-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8ccaf087-1e1b-43f0-9da5-a50e6a539db2/anie202423728-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>DFT computations. Schematic crystal structures of the supercells of a) Na<sub>18</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>36</sub> without Vo and b) Na<sub>18</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>O<sub>35</sub> with Vo. c) Comparison of the average Mn-Mn distance near and far from Vo during the simulated sodiation process from the Na<sub>0</sub> to Na<sub>18</sub> states. Right panel illustrates that the J-T distortion thermodynamically occurs along the direction toward the Vo site. d) Simulations of the mean square displacements of Na ions in the deep sodiated lattice with/without CJTD.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In light of the above experimental and theoretical analyses, the inherent cause of the structural failure of LMROs induced by CJTD and the neutralization effect of intrinsic distortion against J-T distortion are schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0006">6</a>. As for P-NMMO, the collinear J-T effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> gradually forms with the constant reduction of Mn valence upon sodiation, resulting in the cooperative J-T distortion within TM layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0067_R_d113347937e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Sustain.">22</a></sup></span> This CJTD would initially lead to the <i>ab</i> plane twisting and deformation (represented by the P2-P′2 phase transition), simultaneously block the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer in the neighboring Na layers. In this scenario, the Na-transportation blocked regions, together with the serious distortion of the adjacent TM layers, collectively induce severe lattice strain accumulation in the layered oxide. This thermodynamically favors the formation of edge dislocations along the <i>c</i> axis. Consequently, the intragranular cracks would nucleate preferentially in these edge dislocations,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0068_R_d113347937e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Science">23</a></sup></span> thus causing the cathode failure. In the case of D-NMMO, the introduction of Vo leads to the displacements of TM ions near Vo (along the direction away from Vo) due to the reduced electrostatic attraction from oxygen, disrupting the long-range ordering of the <i>ab</i> plane in the pristine state. During the discharge process, the J-T distortion initiates preferentially in the Mn ions near Vo due to their relatively low valence,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423728-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423728-bib-0031_R_d113347937e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span> and the direction of distortion is thermodynamically along the opposite orientation to that of the original TM-ion displacements owing to the low steric hindrance. As a result, the CJTD could be counteracted by the intrinsic distortion to some extent (evidenced by the weaker P′2 phase signals in D-NMMO, Figure <a href="#anie202423728-fig-0004">4b</a>), thus moderating the lattice strain within the <i>ab</i> plane. The small lattice strain (diminished distortion) of the <i>ab</i> plane subsequently ensures the uniform strain distribution along the <i>c</i> axis, which restrains the edge dislocation and crack formation. Consequently, the D-NMMO cathode exhibits a better structural stability and a superior cycling performance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423728-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5a5f2b70-4819-4d87-a7a8-26e1ba6f1412/anie202423728-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5a5f2b70-4819-4d87-a7a8-26e1ba6f1412/anie202423728-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f4b633a9-1c3f-4286-8efc-e2ba6f1bcc96/anie202423728-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5a5f2b70-4819-4d87-a7a8-26e1ba6f1412/anie202423728-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423728-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423728" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustration of the inherent cause of the structural failure of LMROs induced by CJTD and the neutralization effect of intrinsic distortion against J-T distortion.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423728-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423728-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a fire-new “intrinsic distortion against J-T distortion” mechanism in the LMRO cathodes is unraveled, which can substantially improve the structural stability of the layered frameworks during electrochemical cycling. The opposite deformation orientations between the intrinsic distortion and J-T distortion guarantee the small and uniform strain within the <i>ab</i> plane and along the <i>c</i> axis upon sodiation, which effectively alleviates the P2-P′2 phase transition, suppresses the edge dislocation formation, and delays the crack propagation. These merits endow the tailored P2-Na<sub>0.72</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode featuring intrinsic distortion (simply constructed by introducing Vo) with a remarkable cycling durability (91.9 % capacity retention after 500 cycles) while taking full advantage of the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction (186.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.3 C), much superior to the Na-storage performance of the counterpart cathode without intrinsic distortion. This study offers deep and novel insights into the structural failure mechanism of the LMRO cathodes and highlights the significant promise of intrinsic defect engineering for enhancing the cycling stability of the electrodes challenged by J-T effect, including Mn-/Ni-/Fe-based materials, and beyond. We believe that the present fresh and profound perspective on intrinsic defect chemistry broadens the horizons for developing high-energy and long-life cathodes for SIBs and other battery systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
